Diyat — Blood Money
beginner
6 min
Equal Division of Diyah Among Co-Owners of a Shared House
Ruling on equal per capita division of diyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on division of diyah among co-owners of a shared house and explain the analogy to shuf'ah.

Core Idea
In shared house liability, diyah is divided equally per head, not by share size.

When a murder victim is found in a house owned by multiple people with unequal shares, a question arises: how should the blood money (diyah) be divided? This lesson presents Imam Muhammad's ruling that it is divided equally per capita, based on the principle that all co-owners share equal responsibility for management and protection. The ruling is likened to the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah), which also disregards share proportion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The text states that if a murdered person is found in a shared house with unequal shares (e.g., half, one-tenth, and remainder), the diyah is distributed according to the number of heads (ru'us al-rijal) — i.e., equally among all co-owners.
2
Understand the Reasoning
The reason given is that each co-owner, regardless of share size, has equal say in the management (tadbir) of the house. Thus, they are equally responsible for safeguarding it, and equally negligent if a death occurs.
This is a qiyas on the principle of joint liability.
3
Apply the Analogy to Shuf'ah
The text explicitly compares this to shuf'ah (pre-emption), where the right is based on the fact of partnership, not on the proportion of ownership. Just as in shuf'ah all partners have equal rights, here all co-owners bear equal liability.
The analogy reinforces that liability is personal, not proportional.
4
Note the Practical Consequence
The ruling means that a co-owner with a tiny share (e.g., 1/10) pays the same amount as a co-owner with a large share (e.g., 1/2). This is a clear Hanafi position.
Other schools might divide proportionally, but Hanafi insists on equality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشترک گھر میں مقتول پایا جائے تو دیت کے ضمان کا طریقہ
قال فإن وجد القتيل فى دار مشتركة نصفها لرجل وعشرها لرجل ولآخر ما بقى فهو على رؤس الرجال؛ لأن صاحب القليل يزاحم صاحب الكثير فى التدبير فكانوا سواء فى الحفظ والتقصير فيكون على عدد الرؤس
بمنزلة الشفعة
ترجمہ ہم محمد نے فرمایا پس اگر مقتول ایسے مشترک گھر میں پایا جائے کہ اس کا نصف ایک شخص کا ہے اور اس کا عشر ایک کا اور مابقی دوسرے کا ہے تو تاوان رجال کے روس کے مطابق ہو گا اس لئے کہ صاحب قلیل تدبیر میں صاحب کثیر کا مزاحم ہے تو حفاظت و تقصیر میں یہ سب برابر ہوں گے تو تاوان عد در دس پر ہو گا شفعہ کے درجہ میں ۔
تشریح ایک مشترک گھر ہے جس میں مثلا تین آدمیوں کا اشتراک ہے اور ہر ایک کی ملکیت متفاوت ہے برابر نہیں ہے مثلاً زید کا مالک ہے اور بکری کا اور خالدہ کا مالک ہے تو جیسے شفعہ نفس اشتراک کو دیکھتے ہوئے حق شفعہ برابر ملتا ہے مقدار ملکیت کی یجہ سے حق شفعہ میں کمی بیشی نہیں ہوتی اس طرح یہاں بھی مقدار ملک میں تفاوت کی وجہ سے ضمان میں کمی بیش نہیں ہوگی بلکہ نفس
Method of liability for diyah when the murdered person is found in a shared house. He (Muhammad) said: If the murdered person is found in a shared house, half of which belongs to a man, one-tenth to a man, and the remainder to another, then it is upon the heads of the men (i.e., divided per capita), because the owner of a small share competes with the owner of a large share in management, so they are equal in protection and negligence, so it is according to the number of heads, like the case of shuf'ah. Translation: Imam Muhammad said: So if the murdered person is found in such a shared house that half of it is for one person, one-tenth for one, and the remainder for another, then the compensation is according to the heads of the men, because the owner of a small share competes with the owner of a large share in management, so they are all equal in protection and negligence, so the compensation is on the number of heads, like the case of shuf'ah. Explanation: There is a shared house in which, for example, three persons share, and each one's ownership is unequal, not equal. For example, Zayd owns [some], Bakr [some], and Khalid [some]. Then just as in shuf'ah, looking at the fact of partnership, the right of shuf'ah is given equally, not based on the amount of ownership; similarly here, despite the difference in the quantity of ownership, liability does not vary, rather [it is based on] the fact of partnership.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage rules on the distribution of blood money (diyah) when a murder victim is found in a house co-owned by multiple persons with unequal shares (e.g., half, one-tenth, and remainder). Imam Muhammad holds that the liability for diyah is divided equally among all co-owners per capita, not proportionally to their ownership shares. The reasoning is that in management (tadbir) of the house, all owners have equal say regardless of share size, so they are equally responsible for guarding it. This is analogized to pre-emption (shuf'ah), where the right is based on the fact of partnership, not the size of the share. Thus, each owner pays the same amount.

Key Terms
رؤس الرجال (ru'us al-rijal): Per capita division; by number of individuals, not by share amount.
شفعة (shuf'ah): Right of pre-emption; the analogy used here is that the right is based on partnership, not share size.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) According to their shares (50%, 33%, 17%)
B) Divided into three equal parts
C) Only the owner of the largest share pays
D) The government pays

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a murder victim is found in a co-owned house, the diyah is divided equally per capita among all co-owners, irrespective of their ownership shares, based on the principle of equal management responsibility and the analogy of shuf'ah.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Diyah for Body Found in House Before Possession
Intra-Hanafi dispute over whose aqila bears diyah for a body found in a house before possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the basis of the intra-Hanafi dispute over whose aqila bears diyah when a body is found in a purchased house before possession, and apply Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's views to given scenarios.

Core Idea
Liability for a body found on property follows physical possession (yad) according to Abu Hanifa, but ownership (milk) according to the Sahibayn.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute about liability for a dead body found in a house that has been sold but not yet delivered. The passage from Al-Hidayah presents the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa and the differing view of his two major students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding their arguments will sharpen your ability to trace the effective cause (illa) in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputing positions
The case: a house is bought and a dead body is found before the buyer takes possession. Two sub-scenarios: absolute sale (no option) and sale with option for either party. The dispute is between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).
The matn begins with 'قال ومن اشترى دارا...' then 'وقالا' indicating the two views.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling for each scenario
For an absolute sale: the blood money is on the aqila of the seller. For a sale with option: it is on the aqila of the one who currently has physical possession (yad).
Key phrase: 'فهو على عاقلة الذى فى يده'.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling for each scenario
For an absolute sale: the blood money is on the aqila of the buyer. For a sale with option: it is on the aqila of the one for whom ownership ultimately settles (like sadaqah al-fitr).
They use 'قرار الملك' (final settlement of ownership).
4
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa argues that the ability to protect (qudrah 'ala al-hifz) is by physical possession (yad), not ownership. Sahibayn argue that the authority to protect (wilayah al-hifz) is derived from ownership (milk), as seen in the case of a deposit where the depositary is not liable.
The text says: 'وله أن القدرة على الحفظ باليد لا بالملك'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, the financial responsibility for the diyah falls on different aqilas depending on which view is followed. This affects who pays the blood money in real cases.
Understanding this ikhtilaf is important for muftis when issuing fatwas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن اشترى دارا ولم يقبضها حتى وجد فيها قتيل فهو على عاقلة البائع وإن كان في البيع خيار لأحدهما فهو على عاقلة الذى فى يده وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا إن لم يكن فيه خيار فهو على عاقلة المشترى وإن كان فيه خيار فهو على عاقلة الذي تصير له. لأنه إنما أنزل قاتلا باعتبار التقصير فى الحفظ ولا يجب إلا على من له ولاية الحفظ والولاية تستفاد بالملک ولهذا كانت الدية على عاقلة صاحب الدار دون المودع والملك للمشترى قبل القبض في البيع البات وفي المشروط فيه الخيار يعتبر قرار الملک کما فی صدقة الفطر. وله أن القدرة على الحفظ باليد لا بالملك الايرى أنه يقتدر على الحفظ باليد بدون الملك ولا يقتدر بالملك بدون اليد وفي البات اليد للبائع قبل القبض وكذا فيما فيه الخيار لأحدهما قبل القبض؛ لأنه دون البات ولو كان المبيع فى يد المشترى والخيار له فهو أخص الناس به تصرفا ولو كان الخيار للبائع فهو في يده كالمغصوب فتعتبره يده إذ بها يقدر على الحفظ ضمان عليه بالقيمة
He said: And whoever buys a house and does not take possession of it until a dead body is found in it, then it (the blood money) is upon the aqila of the seller. And if in the sale there is an option for one of them, then it is upon the aqila of the one in whose hand it is. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If there is no option in it, then it is upon the aqila of the buyer; and if there is an option in it, then it is upon the aqila of the one for whom the property becomes. Because he has been placed as a killer due to negligence in protection, and it (blood money) is not obligatory except upon one who has authority of protection, and authority is derived from ownership. Hence the blood money is upon the aqila of the owner of the house, not upon the depositary. And ownership is for the buyer before possession in the absolute sale. And in the sale with a condition of option, consideration is given to the final settlement of ownership, as in sadaqah al-fitr. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is: The ability to protect is by hand (possession), not by ownership. Do you not see that he is able to protect by hand without ownership, and he is not able to protect by ownership without hand? And in the absolute sale, the hand is the seller's before possession; likewise in the sale in which there is an option for one of them before possession, because it is less than the absolute sale. And if the sold item is in the hand of the buyer and the option is his, then he is the most specific of people to it in terms of disposal. And if the option is for the seller, then it (the sold item) is in his hand like a usurped item, so his (the buyer's) hand is considered because by it he is able to protect, and he is held liable for it by value.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability for a dead body found in a purchased house before the buyer takes possession. The chapter is about Qasamah (oaths relating to murder), and the issue here is whose aqila (the clan responsible for paying blood money) bears the diyah. The matn from Al-Hidayah states the ruling: According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a person buys a house and a dead body is found before possession, the blood money is on the aqila of the seller (the one who still has possession). If there is a stipulation of option (khayar) for either party, then liability falls on the aqila of the person who currently holds possession. The two companions (Sahibayn—Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) differ: In an absolute sale (no option), the blood money is on the buyer's aqila, because ownership transfers immediately. In a sale with option, they look at where ownership ultimately settles, similar to the rule for sadaqah al-fitr on a sold slave. The commentary then explains the reasoning. The Sahibayn argue that one is considered a killer due to negligence in protection, and the authority to protect is derived from ownership. Therefore, the buyer, as owner, bears responsibility. They analogize to a deposited item: the depositary is not liable; the owner is. Imam Abu Hanifa counters that the ability to protect comes from physical possession (yad), not ownership. One can protect with possession without ownership, but not with ownership without possession. In an absolute sale before delivery, the seller has possession. With an option, the one who has possession is liable. If the buyer has possession and the option, he is the one most able to dispose of it; if the seller has the option, the buyer's possession is like that of a usurper, so his hand is considered sufficient to create liability. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of identifying the effective cause (illa) for liability: Imam focuses on actual control, while the Sahibayn focus on legal ownership.

Key Terms
عاقلة (ʿāqila): The clan or group of male agnates (asabah) who are responsible for paying blood money (diyah) on behalf of a member who has committed manslaughter or where the body is found on their property.
الولاية (al-wilāya): Authority or guardianship; here specifically the authority to protect (wilayah al-hifz) a property, which determines liability for the body found therein.
يد (yad): Hand; in fiqh it signifies physical possession or control over an object. Imam Abu Hanifa considers yad the basis for the ability to protect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, in an absolute sale before possession, whose aqila bears diyah for a dead body found in the house?
A) The seller's aqila
B) The buyer's aqila
C) The aqila of the one in possession
D) No liability

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two positions on this issue: Abu Hanifa prioritizes physical possession (yad), while the Sahibayn prioritize legal ownership (milk).

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
6 min
Proof of Ownership Required for Qasamah and Diyah
Ruling with proof and analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for imposing diyah on the 'aqila when a murdered person is found in a house and apply the analogy to shuf'a.

Core Idea
Mere possession is insufficient; ownership must be proven by witnesses for diyah liability on the 'aqila.

In cases of murder where the killer is unknown, the Hanafi school imposes diyah on the 'aqila of the house owner. But if the 'aqila denies ownership, mere possession is not enough. This passage explains why the school insists on ownership proof, drawing a sharp analogy to the law of pre-emption (shuf'a).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The matn states that if a murdered person is found in a house, the 'aqila will not pay diyah until witnesses testify that the house belongs to the possessor. This is the operative ruling.
The ruling conditions diyah on proven ownership, not mere possession.
2
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The author explains that possession (yad) is only probable evidence of ownership. Because it carries a possibility of being otherwise (like loan or tenancy), it cannot alone obligate financial liability.
The probabilistic nature of yad is key.
3
Trace the analogy to shuf'a
The passage draws a qiyas: just as possession alone does not entitle one to pre-empt a shared property (shuf'a requires ownership), possession alone cannot impose diyah. Both require corroboration.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
If the 'aqila claims the possessor is a borrower or tenant, no diyah is due. But if witnesses confirm his ownership, the 'aqila must pay.
The commentary clarifies the practical consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شخص کے قبضہ میں مکان ہو اور اس میں کوئی مقتول پایا گیا لیکن قابض کی عاقلہ نے اس کی ملکیت کا انکار کر دیا تو دیت کس پر ہے؟
حتى
قال ومن كان في يده دار فوجد فيها قتيل لم تعقله العاقلة تشهد الشهود أنها للذي في يده لأنه لابد من الملك لصاحب اليد حتى تعقل العواقل عنه واليد وإن كانت دليلا على الملك ولكنها محتملة فلا تكفى لإيجاب الدية على العاقلة كما لا تكفى لاستحقاق الشفعة به فى الدار المشفوعة فلابد من إقامة البينة
ترجمہ ام محمد نے فرمایا اور جس کے ہاتھ میں کوئی گھر ہو پس اس میں کوئی مقتول پایا جائے تو عاقلہ اس کی جانب سے دیت نہ دے گا یہاں تک کہ گواہ اس بات کی گواہی دیں کہ یہ دار اس کا ہے جس کے قبضہ میں ہے اس لئے کہ قبضہ والے کی ملکیت کا ہونا ضروری ہے یہاں تک کہ اس کی جانب سے عواقل دیت ادا کریں اور قبضہ اگر چہ ملک پر دلیل ہے لیکن قبضہ متحمل ہے پس وہ عاقلہ پر دیت واجب کرنے کے لئے کافی نہ ہوگا جیسا کہ قبضہ ناکافی ہے اس کے ذریعہ دار مشفوعہ کے اندر شفعہ کے استحقاق کے لئے تو اقامت
تشریح ایک گھر میں مقتول پایا گیا تو حسب بیان مذکور صاحب دار پر قسامت اس کے عاقلہ پر دیت واجب کی گئی مگر عاقلہ نے کہا کہ یہ اس کا مکان نہیں ہے یہ تو اس میں عاریت پر رہتا ہے یا کوئی اور سبب ہے جس کی وجہ سے یہ اس میں رہتا ہے بہر حال یہ اس کا مالک نہیں ہے تو عاقلہ پر دیت واجب نہ ہوگی ہاں اگر گواہوں نے یہ گواہی دیدی کہ یہ مکان اس کی ملکیت ہے جو اس میں رہتا ہے تو عاقلہ پر دیت واجب ہوگی ایسا کیوں؟ اس لئے کہ قابض کے لئے ملکیت بھی درکار ہے تب جا کر عاقلہ پر دیت واجب ہوگا اور اگر چہ قبضہ ظاہرا اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ اس کا مالک قبضہ والا ہے لیکن بہر حال پھر بھی اس میں احتمال دیگر موجود ہے اس لئے خالص قبضہ ایجاب دیت کے لئے کافی نہ ہوگا جیسے خالص قبضہ استحقاق شفعہ کے لئے ناکافی ہوتا ہے تنبیہ ماقبل میں جہاں امام صاحب کا فرمان گذرا ہے کہ قبضہ معتبر ہے تو اس سے مراد وہ قبضہ ہے جو ملکیت کے لئے ہو اسی وجہ سے بالاتفاق مودع پر ضمان واجب نہیں ہے۔
A person has a house in his possession, and a murdered person is found in it, but the possessor's 'aqila denies his ownership. On whom is the diyah? Until: He (Imam Muhammad) said: 'Whoever has a house in his hand, and a murdered person is found therein, the 'aqila will not pay diyah on his behalf until witnesses testify that the house belongs to the one in whose hand it is. Because ownership is necessary for the possessor so that the 'aqila may pay diyah on his behalf. Possession, although it is evidence of ownership, is probable and thus insufficient to obligate diyah on the 'aqila, just as it is insufficient for establishing the right of pre-emption in a shared house. Therefore, proof must be established.' Translation by Umm Muhammad: 'And whoever has a house in his hand, then a murdered person is found therein, the 'aqila will not pay diyah on his behalf until witnesses testify that this house belongs to the one in whose possession it is. This is because possession requires ownership for the 'aqila to pay diyah. Although possession is evidence of ownership, it is probable, so it is not sufficient to obligate diyah on the 'aqila, just as possession is insufficient for the right of pre-emption in a shared house.' Commentary: A murdered person is found in a house; according to the stated explanation, qasamah and diyah were obligated on the 'aqila of the house-owner. But the 'aqila said: 'This is not his house; he lives in it on loan or for some other reason; he is not the owner.' Then diyah will not be obligatory on the 'aqila. However, if witnesses testified that the house is owned by the person living in it, then diyah becomes obligatory on the 'aqila. Why? Because ownership is required for the possessor; only then is diyah obligatory on the 'aqila. Although possession is outwardly evidence that the possessor is the owner, there is still possibility of another reason, so mere possession is insufficient for imposing diyah, just as mere possession is insufficient for the right of pre-emption. Note: Previously, Imam's statement that possession is valid refers to possession linked to ownership; therefore, by consensus, the depositee is not liable.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter of Qasamah. The issue: if a murdered person is found in a house, and the possessor's 'aqila denies his ownership, the diyah is not payable by them until ownership is proven. The author first cites the matn from Imam Muhammad: mere possession (yad) is not sufficient to obligate diyah on the 'aqila; ownership must be established by witnesses. The reasoning is that while possession is a sign of ownership, it is only probable and thus insufficient alone for imposing diyah, analogous to the case of pre-emption (shuf'a) where possession alone does not entitle the possessor to pre-empt. The commentary clarifies that if the 'aqila claims the possessor is a borrower or tenant, no diyah is due. But if witnesses confirm his ownership, diyah becomes obligatory. The tanbih (note) connects this to an earlier point: the earlier statement that possession is valid (mu'tabar) refers to possession accompanied by ownership; hence a depositee is not liable by consensus. This ruling follows the Hanafi position that qasamah and diyah are linked to ownership, not mere custody.

Key Terms
قسامة (qasamah): Oaths taken by the community in case of murder when the killer is unknown; leads to diyah liability.
عاقلة ('aqila): The male agnatic relatives of the killer responsible for paying diyah collectively.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid to the heirs of a murder victim when the killer is not executed.
شفعة (shuf'a): Right of pre-emption for a co-owner to buy out a new partner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school require witnesses to testify that the possessor of a house is its owner before imposing diyah on his 'aqila?
A) Because possession alone is probable evidence and insufficient for financial liability.
B) Because the 'aqila always denies ownership.
C) Because the house belongs to the community.
D) Because the possessor must be a free Muslim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires proven ownership, not mere possession, for diyah to become wajib on the 'aqila in a qasamah case, based on an analogy to shuf'a.

Qasamah for a Murdered Person in a Ship: Possession vs Ownership
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qasamah and diyah for a ship murder.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that possession (yad) determines liability for diyah on mobile property and distinguish it from fixed property where ownership is required.

Core Idea
For movable property like a ship, possession, not ownership, determines liability for diyah.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding qasamah when a murdered person is found in a ship. The central question is whether liability falls on all those present (passengers, sailors, owners) or only on the owner. The passage presents the view of Abu Yusuf and the distinction offered by the two Sahibayn, based on the nature of the property as fixed or movable.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and key authority
Identify the ruling as presented in the matn: all present in the ship (passengers, sailors, owners) are liable for qasamah. Note that this is the view attributed to Abu Yusuf.
Abu Yusuf's view is considered zahir al-riwayah for this scenario.
2
Identify the opposing intra-Hanafi view
Recognize that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) have a distinction: for fixed property (house, locality) ownership matters, but for movable property (ship, animal) possession suffices.
The Sahibayn are the ones making the distinction; their opposition is internal to the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the proof for the distinction
Examine the analogy (qiyas) to an animal (dabba): a ship is movable like an animal, so possession is the criterion, not ownership. By contrast, a house is immovable, so ownership is required.
The reasoning is based on the attribute of transferability (tanqul wa tahawwul).
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Compare the application: in a fixed house, only the owner's 'aqila pays; in a ship, all possessors (including passengers) share liability.
This distinction affects liability in modern contexts like buses, trains, and planes.
5
Identify the tarjih (preferred view)
Check which view is relied upon. The matn and commentary favor Abu Yusuf's view for ships; the Sahibayn's distinction is explanatory but does not override.
The mufta bihi is that all present on a ship are liable.

Chapter of Qasamah. He said: 'If a murdered person is found in a ship, the qasamah is upon those present in it, both passengers and sailors, because it is in their hands. The word 'those in it' includes the owners, so that qasamah is obligated on the owners who are in it, and on the dwellers, and likewise on those who pull it (the ship) – the owner and non-owner are equal. And similarly a cart. This is apparent according to what is narrated from Abu Yusuf. And the distinction for the two (Sahibayn) is that the ship is transferred and moved, so possession is considered, not ownership, as in an animal, unlike the locality and house because they are not transferred.' Translation by Quduri: 'If a murdered person is found in a ship, qasamah is obligatory on those who are in the ship – passengers and sailors – because the ship is in their possession. The word 'those in it' includes the owners, so qasamah is obligatory on the ship-owners who are in it, and on the dwellers, and likewise on those who pull the ship – owner and non-owner are equal, and similarly a cart. This is apparent according to Abu Yusuf's narration. And the distinction for the two is that the ship is transferred and moved, so possession is considered, not ownership, as in an animal, unlike the locality and house because they are not transferred.' Commentary: If a murdered person is found in a ship, qasamah and diyah are obligatory on all those in the ship, whether passengers, owners, or sailors – all are liable. Similarly a bullock cart; thus also buses. Question: The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said in the previous lesson that only the owners are liable, not the dwellers. However, according to Abu Yusuf, dwellers and owners are equal. How is this ruling consensual? Answer: Abu Yusuf's reason is apparent; he follows his earlier principle. As for the objection against the two (Sahibayn), they respond that a house and locality are not transferred, while a ship is transferred and moved, so like an animal, ownership is not considered, but possession is considered. And possession of the ship belongs to all – passengers, owners, or sailors.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute within the chapter of Qasamah. The issue: if a murdered person is found in a ship, who is liable for qasamah and diyah? The matn states that all present (passengers and sailors) are liable because the ship is in their possession. The phrase 'those in it' includes the owners. The commentary clarifies that Abu Yusuf's view extends liability equally to owners, dwellers, and even those who pull the ship (tugboats), and analogizes to a cart. The two (Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold a distinction: for movable objects like a ship or animal, possession (yad) suffices for liability, not ownership, unlike a fixed house or locality where ownership is required. The question and answer section explains that Abu Yusuf's position follows his consistent principle, while the response to the objection against the Sahibayn is that the mobile nature of the ship demands a ruling based on custodial possession rather than ownership.

Key Terms
سفينة (safinah): Ship or large boat; treated as movable property for qasamah.
عجلة ('ajalah): Cart (bullock cart or any vehicle) - analogous to ship for qasamah.
يد (yad): Possession; in this context, physical control of the movable object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because passengers are temporary owners.
D) Because the ship is owned by the community.

Key Takeaway
For movable property like ships and carts, possession (yad) determines liability for diyah, not ownership.

Ruling on Qasamah for Murder in a Neighborhood Mosque
Ruling statement on qasamah for a neighborhood mosque.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qasamah for a murder in a neighborhood mosque and explain why liability falls on the residents.

Core Idea
Liability for murder in a local mosque falls on its managing residents.

The ruling on qasamah (oaths for retribution/diyat) when a murdered person is found in a place of worship depends on who is responsible for that mosque's management. This lesson focuses on a specific case: when the murder occurs in a mosque belonging to a particular neighborhood (masjid mahallah). Understanding this rule helps clarify the broader principle of liability in Hanafi criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State clearly what the passage says: qasamah and diyah are upon the people of the neighborhood.
The ruling is explicit in the matn.
2
Determine the Reason
The reason given is 'al-tadbir fihi ilayhim' – management of the mosque is in their hands.
Tadbir is the key concept linking authority and liability.
3
Distinguish from Other Mosques
This ruling applies specifically to a masjid mahallah, not the grand congregational mosque (jami).
The greater mosque is managed by the city, so liability differs.
4
Apply the Principle
Liability follows responsibility: those who maintain and secure the mosque must answer for crimes within.
This is a general Hanafi principle.
5
Note the Authority
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah (Quduri's matn) and is mufta bihi.
It is a standard relied-upon position.

Source Text

Commentary: The preservation of the neighborhood mosque is the duty of the people of the neighborhood; therefore, if a murdered person is found in the neighborhood mosque, qasamah and diyah are obligatory on the people of the neighborhood.

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the chapter of Qasamah. It states that if a murdered person is found in a neighborhood mosque, the qasamah and diyah are upon the residents of that neighborhood. The reason given is that the management (tadbir) of the mosque is entrusted to them. The commentary explains that since the residents are responsible for the mosque's upkeep and security, they bear liability for any murder occurring there. This is based on the principle that those responsible for a place's maintenance are answerable for crimes within it when the perpetrator is unknown.

Key Terms

تدبیر (tadbir): Management and oversight; in this context, the responsibility for the mosque's affairs.

Ruling on Qasamah for a Murdered Person Found in Public Places
Ruling statement on qasamah for public places.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qasamah when a murdered person is found in a public place (congregational mosque or main street) and explain why the diyah is paid from the public treasury.

Core Idea
No qasamah for murders in public spaces; diyah falls on the state treasury.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in the law of qasamah (collective oath-taking) for homicide. The Hanafi school holds that if a murdered body is discovered in a location that belongs to the general public — such as the main congregational mosque or a major public street — the procedure of qasamah is dropped entirely. Instead, the blood money (diyah) is paid from the public treasury (bayt al-mal). Understanding this ruling highlights how property ownership and public access affect criminal liability in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the place
The ruling applies to two specific public places: al-masjid al-jami' (congregational mosque) and al-shari' al-a'zam (main street). Note that these are not privately owned or exclusive to any group.
2
State the ruling
For a body found in such a place, there is no qasamah (saaqit). That means no group from the locality is required to take fifty oaths.
3
Identify the financial consequence
Instead of qasamah, the diyah is paid from bayt al-mal (the public treasury of the Islamic state).
4
Understand the rationale
The reason given in the text is 'because it is for the general public and is not exclusive to anyone' (li-annahu li-l-'ammah la yakhtassu bi-hi). Since the space belongs to everyone, no specific community can be held responsible for security, so the state bears the cost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جامع مسجد یا شارع عام پر پائے جانے والے مقتول کی قسامت ساقط ہے اور دیت بیت المال پر ہے
وإن وجد في المسجد الجامع أو الشارع الأعظم فلا قسامة فيه والدية على بيت المال لأنه للعامة لا يختص به
The qasamah for a murdered person found in the congregational mosque or the main street is dropped, and the diyah is upon the public treasury. 'And if a murdered person is found in the congregational mosque or the main street, then there is no qasamah in it, and the diyah is upon the public treasury (bayt al-mal), because it is for the general public and is not exclusive to anyone.'
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This is a ruling statement concluding the chapter. If a murdered person is found in the grand congregational mosque of a city or in the main public street, no qasamah is imposed on any specific group. Instead, the diyah is paid from the public treasury (bayt al-mal). The reason is that these places are public property, not exclusively managed by any particular group of people. Therefore, no one individual or community can be held responsible for the security of these spaces, and the state treasury bears the cost.

Key Terms
مسجد جامع (masjid jami'): The main congregational mosque of a city where Friday prayers are held.
شارع أعظم (shari' a'zam): The main public street or highway.
بیت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Islamic state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The residents of the nearest mosque are liable.
B) The diyah is paid from the public treasury.
C) The city ruler is personally liable.
D) No diyah is paid at all.

Key Takeaway
Qasamah is dropped when a murder victim is found in a congregational mosque or main street; the diyah is paid from the public treasury because the place belongs to the general public.

Blood-Money for a Murder in Prison: Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyah liability in prison murder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on diyah liability for a murder victim found in prison and evaluate the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Prisoners are powerless, so the public treasury, not they, bears blood-money.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa (along with Muhammad al-Shaybani) and Abu Yusuf regarding who is liable for the blood-money (diyah) when a murder victim is found inside a prison. The dispute hinges on whether the prisoners, as occupants of the prison, can be held responsible for the failure to protect, or whether the loss should be borne by the public treasury because the prison benefits all Muslims. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the Hanafi principles of liability (damān) in complex scenarios.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify what each party says about who pays diyah: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say Bayt al-Mal pays; Abu Yusuf says the prisoners pay.
2
Analyze Abu Yusuf's evidence
Understand his reasoning: prisoners are the occupants (sakin) with management authority (wilayat al-tadbir), so they are liable for failure to protect.
3
Analyze the Tarafayn's evidence
Understand their reasoning: prisoners are maqhur (powerless) and cannot help; the prison serves public benefit, so the loss falls on Bayt al-Mal.
4
Identify the underlying principle
Recognize that this case is a branch of the owner vs. occupant liability rule. Abu Yusuf holds the occupant liable; the Tarafayn hold the owner liable.
The prisoners are occupants but not owners, so each position follows logically.

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۶ ۴۷ باب القسامة قید خانہ والوں پر واجب ہے اس لئے کہ مکان وہی ہیں اور تدبیر کی ولایت انہی کی جانب ہے اور ظاہر یہ ہے کہ قبل انہی کی جانب سے حاصل ہوا ہے اور طرفین فرماتے ہیں کہ قیدی لوگ مقہور ہیں تو وہ مدد نہیں کر سکتے تو ان کے ساتھ وہ حکم متعلق نہ ہوگا جو نصرت کی وجہ سے واجب ہوتا ہے اور اس لئے کہ قید خانہ بنایا گیا ہے مسلمانوں کے حقوق کو وصول کرنے کے لئے پس جب اس کی منفعت مسلمانوں کی جانب لوٹتی ہے تو اس کا تاوان بھی انہی کی جانب کوئے گا مشائخ نے فرمایا اور یہ مسئلہ مالک وساکن کی فرع ہے اور وہ مسئلہ مختلف فیہ ہے ابوحنیفہ اور ابو یوسف کے درمیان۔ تشریح اگر مقتول قید خانے میں ملے تو حضرات طرفین کے نزدیک اس کی دیت بیت المال پر واجب ہوگی اور امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک اس کی دیت قیدیوں پر واجب ہوگی۔ امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ قسامت ودیت ان کے اصول کے مطابق کان پر واجب ہوتی ہے اور قید خانے کے مکان قیدی ہیں لہذا تد بیر کی ولایت انہی کو حاصل ہوگی اور ظاہر بھی یہی ہے کہ قبل انہی کی جانب سے متحقق ہوا ہے۔ حضرات طرفین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ قیدی لوگ مقہور اور مغلوب ہیں وہ کیا کسی کی امداد کر سکتے ہیں وہ خود ہی مستحق نصرت و امداد ہیں لہذا ترک نصرت کی وجہ سے جو قسامت و دیت واجب ہوتی ہے وہ ان پر واجب نہ ہوگی اور دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ قید خانہ اس لئے بنایا گیا ہے تاکہ مسلمانوں کے حقوق محفوظ رہ سکیں اور وصول ہو سکیں۔ خلاصہ کلام . قید خانے کی منفعت عامتہ المسلمین کو پہنچتی ہے لہذا اس کا غرم و تاوان بھی مسلمانوں ہی کو پہنچے گا اورانہی کے مال میں دیت واجب ہوگی اور ان کا مال وہ بیت المال کا مال ہے۔ در حقیقت یہ مسئلہ ماقبل ذکر کردہ مسئلہ کی فرع ہے یعنی ابو یوسف کے نزدیک ساکن پر ضمان ہے اور طرفین کے نزدیک مالک پر اور قیدی قید خانہ میں ساکن تو ہیں لیکن مالک نہیں ہیں لہذا بقول ابو یوسف ان پر ضمان ہو گا اور بقول طرفین ان پر ضمان نہ ہوگا ۔
Ashraf al-Hidaya, Urdu commentary on Hidaya, Volume 16, Page 47, Chapter of Qasamah. It is obligatory upon the prison inmates because they are the occupants and the authority of management is in their hands, and it is apparent that the killing occurred from their side. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) say that the prisoners are oppressed, so they cannot help, so the ruling that is obligatory due to support will not apply to them, and because the prison was built for the recovery of Muslims' rights. So when its benefit returns to the Muslims, then its compensation also goes to them. The scholars said: This issue is a branch of the issue of owner and occupant, and that issue is disputed between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Commentary: If the victim is found in a prison, according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) his blood-money is obligatory on the Bayt al-Mal (public treasury), and according to Imam Abu Yusuf, his blood-money is obligatory on the prisoners. Abu Yusuf's proof: Qasamah and diyah are obligatory on the place according to their principles, and the places of the prison are the prisoners, so the authority of management belongs to them, and it is apparent that the killing occurred from them. The proof of the two: The prisoners are oppressed and subdued, they cannot help anyone; they themselves are deserving of help. Therefore, the qasamah and diyah which become obligatory due to leaving support will not be obligatory on them. Second reason: The prison was built to protect and recover Muslims' rights. In summary, the benefit of the prison reaches the general public of Muslims, so its loss and compensation also go to the Muslims, and their wealth is the wealth of the Bayt al-Mal. In fact, this issue is a branch of the previously mentioned issue: according to Abu Yusuf, the occupant is liable, and according to the two, the owner is liable. The prisoners are occupants of the prison but not owners. Therefore, according to Abu Yusuf, they will be liable, and according to the two, they will not be liable.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa (along with Imam Muhammad) and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding who bears the blood-money (diyah) and qasamah for a murder victim found inside a prison. The text first states the ruling that it is obligatory upon the prisoners, then gives the opposing view. Abu Yusuf argues that the prisoners are the occupants (sakin) of the prison and have management authority (wilayat al-tadbir), so they are responsible for protection and thus liable. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) counter that prisoners are powerless (maqhur) and cannot provide help; they are themselves in need of assistance, so liability for failure to protect does not apply. Additionally, the prison serves the public interest of Muslims, and its benefit returns to the Muslim community, so the loss should be borne by the Bayt al-Mal. The commentator clarifies that this issue is a branch of a general rule: Abu Yusuf holds the occupant (sakin) liable for damages, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the owner (malik) liable. Since prisoners are occupants but not owners, each position follows logically.

Key Terms
قسامت (qasāmah): An oath taken by the relatives of a murder victim or by the accused in cases where the murderer is unknown, often associated with blood-money.
دیت (diyah): Blood-money, a financial compensation paid to the heirs of a murder victim.
بیت المال (bayt al-māl): The public treasury of the Islamic state, used for public expenses and compensation in certain cases.
مقہور (maqhur): Subdued or oppressed; unable to exercise authority or provide support.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf, who is liable for the blood-money of a murder victim found in a prison?
A) The prisoners
B) The Bayt al-Mal
C) The prison guards
D) The victim's family

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that the public treasury, not the prisoners, bears blood-money for a murder in prison because prisoners are powerless and the prison serves the public interest.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Murder in Non-Owned Wilderness: Blood is Wasted
Ruling on blood-money for murder in wilderness far from habitation or on owned land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a murder victim's blood is considered hadar (wasted) and apply the ruling to determine liability for diyah.

Core Idea
Blood is wasted only if the wilderness is far from habitation and unowned; if owned, the owner's clan pays diyah.

Al-Quduri presents a ruling on murder in a wilderness that hinges on two key factors: proximity to habitation and land ownership. The Hanafi school defines proximity by audibility of sound, not distance. Understanding this ruling helps you distinguish between cases where blood-money is payable and where it is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct situations: (1) wilderness far from habitation and not owned, and (2) wilderness that is owned. Liability differs.
2
Apply the definition of qurb
Proximity is defined by whether a person's voice can be heard from the nearest habitation. If help cannot reach, no negligence is attributed.
This is a Hanafi-specific measure; other schools may use distance.
3
Determine ownership
If the wilderness is owned by someone, the owner's 'āqilah (agnatic clan) is responsible for diyah and qasamah, because the owner has control over the land.
4
Conclude the ruling
If both conditions are met (far from habitation and unowned), blood is hadar (wasted) with no liability. Otherwise, diyah is due on the owner's 'āqilah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير مملوك جنگل میں پائے جانے والے مقتول کی دیت و قسامت
قال وإن وجد في برية ليس بقربها عمارة فهو هدر وتفسير القرب ما ذكرنا من استماع الصوت؛ لأنه إذا كان بهذه الحالة لا يلحقه الغوث من غيره فلايوصف أحد بالتقصير وهذا إذا لم تكن مملوكة لأحد أما إذا كانت فالدية والقسامة على عاقلته
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور اگر مقتول کسی جنگل میں ملے جس کے قریب کوئی آبادی نہ ہو تو وہ رائیگاں ہے اور قرب کی تفسیر وہی ہے جو ہم ذکر کر چکے ہیں یعنی آواز کو سننا اس لئے کہ مقتول جب اس حال پر ہو گا تو اس کو اس کا غیر کوئی فریاد رس نہیں پہنچ سکے گا تو کوئی تقصیر سے متصف نہ ہوگا اور یہ حکم اس وقت ہے جب کہ وہ جنگل کسی کا مملوک نہ ہو بہر حال جب وہ کسی کا مملوک ہو تو دیت اور قسامت مالک جنگل کے عاقلہ پر ہوگی۔ تشریح اگر مقتول کسی ایسے جنگل میں ملے جو کسی کا مملوک نہ ہو تو کسی پر قسامت و دیت نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کا خون ہدر ہے بشرطیکہ اس جنگل کے پاس کوئی آبادی نہ ہو کہ جس کے پاس یہاں سے اس آبادی میں آواز پہنچ سکے کیونکہ جب اس کے قریب کوئی ایسی آبادی نہیں ہے تو اس کے پاس کوئی فریا درس بھی نہیں آسکتا اور نہ آنے میں کسی کی جانب سے تقصیر نہیں ہے لیکن اگر یہ جنگل کسی کا مملوک ہو تو پھر اس جنگل کے مالک کے عاقلہ پر دیت واجب ہوگی ۔
Blood-money and qasamah for a victim found in a non-owned forest. He (al-Quduri) said: If he is found in a wilderness near which there is no habitation, it is wasted, and the interpretation of proximity is what we mentioned of hearing the sound, because if he is in this state, help from others cannot reach him, so no one is described as negligent. This is if it is not owned by anyone. But if it is owned, then the blood-money and qasamah are upon his 'āqilah (the male relatives on the father's side who pay blood-money). Translation: Al-Quduri said: If the victim is found in a forest near which there is no habitation, then it is wasted. The interpretation of proximity is what we mentioned: hearing the sound. Because when the victim is in this state, no one else can reach him to help, so no one is described as having negligence. This ruling is for when the forest is not owned by anyone. However, when it is owned, the blood-money and qasamah are upon the 'āqilah of the forest owner. Commentary: If the victim is found in a forest that is not owned, then no one has qasamah or diyah; rather his blood is wasted, provided there is no habitation near the forest from which his voice could be heard. Because when there is no such habitation nearby, no helper can come to him, and there is no negligence from anyone. But if this forest is owned by someone, then the blood-money is obligatory upon the 'āqilah of the forest owner.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from al-Quduri regarding a murder victim found in a wilderness. The key condition is proximity to habitation (qurb), defined by whether a person's voice can be heard from that location. If the wilderness is far from habitation such that help cannot arrive, and the land is not privately owned, then the blood is wasted (hadar) — no one is liable. However, if the wilderness is owned by someone, the owner's 'āqilah (tribal group liable for blood-money) is responsible for the diyah and qasamah, because the owner has control over the land and thus a duty to protect those on it. The commentator emphasizes that the definition of qurb is based on audibility of sound, not distance in kilometers.

Key Terms
ہدر (hadar): Wasted; no blood-money or qisas is payable; the killing is effectively ignored in terms of financial liability.
بریہ (barriyah): A wilderness or desert area far from human habitation.
استماع الصوت (istimā' al-ṣawt): Hearing the sound; the criterion for qurb (proximity) — if the victim's shouts can be heard in the nearest habitation.
عاقلہ (āqilah): The group of male agnatic relatives (asabah) who are responsible for paying blood-money (diyah) on behalf of the killer or landowner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the blood of a murder victim found in a forest considered hadar (wasted)?
A) When the forest is owned
B) When the forest is far from habitation and not owned
C) Always
D) When the victim is unknown

Key Takeaway
A murder victim's blood is wasted only if found in a wilderness far from habitation (voice not heard) and the land is not owned; otherwise, diyah is due on the owner's 'āqilah.

Murder Between Two Villages or in the Middle of a River
Ruling on liability for murder between two villages or in a flowing river

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability for blood-money when a murder victim is found between two villages or in a non-owned flowing river, applying the Hanafi definition of proximity.

Core Idea
Blood-money is due from the nearer village, but no liability if the victim is found in the middle of a non-owned river.

This passage from al-Quduri's matn addresses two situations where a murder victim is found without an identified killer. The key question is who bears financial responsibility (diyah and qasamah). The Hanafi ruling hinges on proximity to inhabited areas or possession of the place where the body is found.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two distinct cases: (1) victim found between two villages, (2) victim found in the middle of a flowing river (Euphrates).
2
Determine the rule for each scenario
For between villages: blood-money (diyah) and qasamah are wajib upon the nearer village. For river middle: no liability (blood is wasted).
3
Understand the definition of proximity
'Nearer' (aqrab) is defined by the distance from which a voice can be heard (istimā' al-ṣawt). The nearer village had a greater duty to protect that area.
Reference to previous explanation in the text.
4
Understand the rationale for no liability in river
The river is not in anyone's hand or ownership (yad or milk). Therefore, no one can be held negligent for failing to prevent the murder.
The Euphrates is an example; applies to any large non-owned flowing river.
5
Note the generic application of 'Euphrates'
The commentator clarifies that 'Euphrates' (furāt) symbolizes any river not privately owned. The ruling is not limited to that specific river.
Avoids the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دو گاؤں کے درمیان مقتول ملے تو دیت و قسامت کس پر ہے
وإن وجد بين قريتين كان على أقربهما وقد بيناه وإن وجد في وسط الفرات يمر به الماء فهو هدر لأنه ليس في يد أحد ولا في ملكه
ترجمہ اور اگر مقتول دوبستیوں کے درمیان ملے تو وجوب دیت ان میں سے اقرب پر ہے اور ہم اس کو بیان کر چکے ہیں اور اگر متقول دریائے فرات کے درمیان میں ملے جس کو پانی بہائے لیجاتا ہے تو یہ بدر ہے اس لئے کہ فرات کسی کے قبضہ میں نہیں ہے اور نہ کسی کی ملکیت میں ہے۔ تشریح اگر مقتول دو گاؤں کے بیچ میں ملے جو اس جگہ سے قریب ہو گا اس کے باشندوں پر قسامت و دبیت واجب ہوگی جس کی وجہ ماقبل میں گذر چکی ہے اور اگر مقتول دریائے فرات کے کنارے پر نہیں بلکہ وسط میں ملے جس کو پانی کی دھار بہا کر لے جارہی ہے تو اس کی دیت کسی پر بھی واجب نہ ہوگی کیونکہ وہ کسی کے قبضہ میں نہیں۔ اور فرات کے تحت میں ہر وہ دریا داخل ہے جو کسی کی ملکیت میں نہ ہو۔
If a victim is found between two villages, then the blood-money is upon the nearer of them, and we have explained it. And if he is found in the middle of the Euphrates, which water flows past, then it is wasted, because it is not in anyone's hand nor in his ownership. Translation: If the victim is found between two settlements, the obligation of blood-money is upon the nearer one, and we have explained that. And if the victim is found in the middle of the river Euphrates, which water carries away, then it is wasted, because the Euphrates is not in anyone's possession nor in anyone's ownership. Commentary: If the victim is found between two villages, the qasamah and diyah are obligatory upon the residents of the one that is nearer to that place, for the reason that passed previously. And if the victim is found not on the riverbank but in the middle of the river, being carried by the current, then no one is liable for his blood-money, because it is in no one's possession. And under 'Euphrates' is included every river that is not owned by anyone.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct scenarios. First, if a murder victim is found between two villages, the blood-money and qasamah are due from the nearer village. The definition of proximity (qurb) has already been explained as the distance from which a voice can be heard; the nearer village had a greater duty to protect that area. Second, if the victim is found in the middle of a large flowing river like the Euphrates, no one is liable because the river is in no one's possession or ownership. The blood is wasted (hadar). The commentator clarifies that 'Euphrates' here symbolizes any large river not privately owned.

Key Terms
قرب (qurb): Proximity to the location where the victim is found, measured by audibility of sound (istimā' al-ṣawt).
فرات (furāt): The Euphrates River, used here as an example of a large flowing river that is not privately owned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a murder victim is found in the middle of a large flowing river that is not owned by anyone, who is liable for the blood-money according to the Hanafi school?
A) The nearest village
B) The owner of the river
C) No one (blood is wasted)
D) The government

Key Takeaway
Liability for diyah and qasamah depends on whether the location is in someone's possession or within the protective proximity of a village; non-owned flowing water absolves all parties of liability.

Diyat — Blood Money

Murder Victim Entangled on a Riverbank: Liability of the Nearest Village
Ruling on blood-money for murder victim entangled on riverbank

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on blood-money liability for a murder victim found entangled on a riverbank and explain the basis of possession through customary use.

Core Idea
The nearest village is liable for murder on the riverbank because customary use establishes possession and a duty to protect.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from al-Hidayah: a murder victim is found entangled on the bank of a large river. Who bears the responsibility for blood-money? The Hanafi answer hinges on the concept of possession (yad) derived from customary use. We will also contrast this with a privately owned stream to clarify the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The victim is found 'entangled on the bank' (muḥtabisan bil-shāṭi') of a large river, not in the water itself.
The Urdu commentary uses 'الجھا ہوا' (entangled).
2
Determine the liable party
The nearest village (aqrab al-qurā) to that spot is liable for blood-money (diyat) and qasamah.
This is based on the interpretation of proximity (qurb) already explained.
3
Understand the legal basis
The riverbank is effectively in the possession (yad) of the nearest villagers because they draw water from it and water their cattle there. This possession creates a duty to protect the area, and their negligence makes them liable.
The Arabic phrase 'ألا ترى' (do you not see?) is used to highlight this customary evidence.
4
Distinguish from private ownership
The passage contrasts a small stream where pre-emption (shuf'ah) is claimed. Such a stream is clearly in the possession of its owners, and if a victim is found there, the owners—not the nearest village—are liable.
Shuf'ah indicates stronger property rights than mere customary use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بڑے دریا کے کنارے سے الجھے ہوئے مقتول کی دیت و قسامت
وإن كان محتبساً بالشاطيء فهو على أقرب القرى من ذلك المكان على التفسير الذي تقدم لأنه أخص بنصرة هذا الموضع فهو كالموضوع على الشط والشط في يد من هو بقرب منه ألا ترى أنهم يستقون منه الماء ویوردون بهائمهم فيها بخلاف النهر الذى يستحق به الشفعة لاختصاص أهلها به لقيام يدهم عليه فتكون القسامة والدية عليهم.
ترجمہ و إن كان محتبساً بالشاطيء  اور اگر مقتول فرات کے کنارے سے الجھا ہوا ہو تو وہ ( وجوب دیت ) اس جگہ قریبی گاؤں والوں پر ہو گا قرب کی اس تفسیر کے مطابق جو ماقبل میں گزر چکی ہے اس لئے کہ وہی ( اقرب القرمی اس جگہ کی نصرت کے ساتھ مخصوص ہے تو مقتول ایسا ہو گیا جیسے کنارے پر رکھا ہوا اور کنارہ ان لوگوں کے قبضہ میں ہے جو اس سے قریب میں کیا آپ نہیں دیکھتے کہ وہ اہل اقرب القریٰ اس سے پانی بھرتے ہیں اور اس میں اپنے چوپاؤں کو لاتے ہیں بخلاف اس نہر کے جس کے ذریعہ شفعہ کا استحقاق ہوتا ہے شفعہ کے مختص ہونے کی وجہ سے اس نہر کے ساتھ اس نہر پر ان کا قبضہ ہو نیکی وجہ سے تو قسامت اور دیت انہی پر ہوگی۔ تشریح اگر مقتول بڑے دریا کے کنارے سے الجھا ہوا ہے تو جو بستی اس جگہ کے قریب ہوگی انہی پر دیت واجب ہوگی کیونکہ اس جگہ کی نصرت انہیں کا فریضہ ہے تو ان کی جانب سے تقصیر کا صدور ہوا ہے اس لئے وہ دیت کے ذمہ دار ہوں گے اور قرب کی تفسیر ماقبل میں گذر چکی ہے تو اب اس مقتول کا حکم ایسا ہو گیا جیسے دریا کے کنارے پر پڑے ہوئے کا اور کنارہ چونکہ اس بستی والوں کے قبضہ میں ہے کیونکہ وہ اس کنارے سے پانی لیتے ہیں اور اپنے چوپاؤں کو یہاں لاتے ہیں ہاں اگر چھوٹی نہر ہو جس میں شرکت کی وجہ سے شفعہ کا استحقاق ہوتا ہے تو وہ نہر اس کے اہل کے قبضہ میں ہے لہذا اگر اس میں کوئی مقتول میلا تو اس کی دیت انہی پر واجب ہوگی اور انہی پر
Blood-money and qasamah for a victim entangled on the bank of a large river. And if he is entangled on the bank, then it is upon the nearest village to that place, according to the interpretation which preceded, because they are more specific in assisting this place, so it is as if placed on the bank, and the bank is in the hand of those who are near it. Do you not see that they draw water from it and bring their cattle to it, contrary to a stream for which pre-emption (shuf'ah) is claimed due to the special attachment of its people to it because of their hand being upon it? So the qasamah and diyah are upon them. Translation: And if the victim is entangled on the bank of the Euphrates, then the obligation of blood-money is upon the nearest village, according to the interpretation of proximity that passed earlier, because they are specifically responsible for assisting this place. So the victim becomes as if placed on the bank, and the bank is in the possession of those who are near it. Do you not see that the people of the nearest village draw water from it and bring their cattle to it, unlike a stream for which pre-emption is claimed due to the special attachment of its people to it because of their possession? So the qasamah and diyah are upon them. Commentary: If the victim is entangled on the bank of a large river, then the settlement that is near that place will have the blood-money obligatory upon them, because the assistance of that place is their duty, so negligence occurred from their side, therefore they are responsible for the blood-money. The interpretation of proximity has passed previously. So now the ruling of this victim becomes like one placed on the riverbank, and the bank is in the possession of those villagers because they draw water from it and bring their cattle here. However, if it is a small stream regarding which pre-emption is claimed due to partnership, then that stream is in the possession of its partners, so if a victim is found in it, the blood-money is upon them and upon them.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage deals with a murder victim found entangled on the bank of a large river. The ruling is that the nearest village is liable for the blood-money and qasamah. The reason is that the riverbank is effectively in the possession (yad) of the nearest villagers through customary use: they draw water from it and water their cattle there. Thus, they have a duty to protect that area, and their failure results in liability. The commentator contrasts this with a small privately-owned stream where pre-emption (shuf'ah) rights exist, indicating clearer possession; in that case, the owners are liable. The definition of proximity (qurb) remains the same: the distance from which a voice can be heard.

Key Terms
محتبساً بالشاطيء (muḥtabisan bil-shāṭi'): Entangled on the bank; specifically a murder victim caught or lodged on the riverbank.
شط (shaṭṭ): Riverbank or shore; the land adjacent to a river.
شفعة (shuf'ah): Pre-emption; the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a share in property before outsiders.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the nearest village have possession of the riverbank according to the passage?
A) They own the land officially
B) They draw water from it and water their cattle there
C) They have pre-emption rights
D) The river belongs to them

Key Takeaway
Liability for murder on a riverbank falls on the nearest village due to their customary possession and duty of protection.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Diyat for a Body Found Between Villages or in a Non-Owned River
Ruling on diyah liability for a body found between villages or in a non-owned river

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that diyat is due from the nearest locality when a body is found between villages, and explain why no diyat is due for a body found in the middle of a non-owned river.

Core Idea
Blood money follows control: if the body is in a place under no one's possession, the blood is wasted.

This passage addresses two scenarios of diyat liability for a found dead body. The author first confirms the rule for bodies found between two villages—liability falls on the nearer village. Then he turns to the case of a body floating in a large, non-owned river like the Euphrates. Here, because the river is not in anyone's hand or ownership, no blood money is due. Understanding the reasoning (talil) behind these rulings is essential for applying similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two distinct situations: (1) body found between two villages, (2) body found in the middle of a non-owned river (like the Euphrates).
The phrase 'بين قريتين' vs 'في وسط الفرات' marks the scenarios.
2
Extract the ruling for each
For the first scenario, the ruling is that the nearer village bears the diyat. For the second, the blood is 'hadar' (wasted) – no diyat is due.
The author also references a prior explanation (قد بيناه) for the village case.
3
Locate the proof (talil)
Focus on the reason given: 'لأنه ليس في يد أحد ولا في ملكه' – because it is not in anyone's hand or ownership. This is the effective cause (illah) for negating liability.
This is a qiyas-based reasoning: absence of possession (yad) removes liability.
4
Apply the underlying principle
Understand that liability in qasamah/diyat is tied to de facto control. If a body is found in a place under someone's control, they become the prime suspects. Without control, no one is held liable.
This principle can be extended to other bodies found in ownerless places.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن وجد بين قريتين كان على أقربهما وقد بيناه وإن وجد في وسط الفرات يمر به الماء فهو هدر لأنه ليس في يد أحد ولا في ملكه
ترجمہ اور اگر مقتول دوبستیوں کے درمیان ملے تو وجوب دیت ان میں سے اقرب پر ہے اور ہم اس کو بیان کر چکے ہیں اور اگر متقول دریائے فرات کے درمیان میں ملے جس کو پانی بہائے لیجاتا ہے تو یہ بدر ہے اس لئے کہ فرات کسی کے قبضہ میں نہیں ہے اور نہ کسی کی ملکیت میں ہے۔ تشریح اگر مقتول دو گاؤں کے بیچ میں ملے جو اس جگہ سے قریب ہو گا اس کے باشندوں پر قسامت و دبیت واجب ہوگی جس کی وجہ ماقبل میں گذر چکی ہے اور اگر مقتول دریائے فرات کے کنارے پر نہیں بلکہ وسط میں ملے جس کو پانی کی دھار بہا کر لے جارہی ہے تو اس کی دیت کسی پر بھی واجب نہ ہوگی کیونکہ وہ کسی کے قبضہ میں نہیں۔ اور فرات کے تحت میں ہر وہ دریا داخل ہے جو کسی کی ملکیت میں نہ ہو۔
And if (the dead body) is found between two villages, it is upon the nearer of them, and we have already explained it. And if it is found in the middle of the Euphrates, water passing by it, then it is wasted, because it is not in anyone's hand nor in anyone's ownership. Translation: And if the slain is found between two towns, the obligation of blood money is upon the nearer of them, and we have already explained it. And if the slain is found in the middle of the Euphrates River, which water carries away, then it is wasted, because the Euphrates is not in anyone's possession nor in anyone's ownership. Explanation: If the slain is found between two villages, the inhabitants of the nearer place will be obligated for qasamah and blood money, as previously explained. And if the slain is found not on the bank of the Euphrates but in the middle, carried away by the current, then no blood money is due upon anyone, because it is not in anyone's possession. And under 'Euphrates' is included every river that is not owned by anyone.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for a dead body discovered between two villages or in the middle of a non-owned river. For the two-village case, the author refers to a prior explanation: the closer village bears the responsibility of qasamah and diyah, based on the presumption that the killer is from among them and they failed to prevent the crime. For the river case, if the body is found floating in the middle of a large, non-owned river like the Euphrates, no blood money is due, because the river is not under the possession or ownership of any specific community. The word 'هدر' means the blood is wasted—no liability falls on anyone. The condition for liability is that the body must be found in a place under someone's de facto control (يد). This is the reasoning (talil) provided: absence of possession negates liability. The commentary extends the ruling to all similar non-owned rivers.

Key Terms
القسامة (al-qasāmah): Oath compurgation: a procedure where fifty men from the locality swear oaths to deny knowledge of the killer, and if they refuse, they pay blood money.
هدر (hadar): Wasted, no blood money is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a dead body is found floating in the middle of the Euphrates River, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Blood money is due from the nearest village.
B) Blood money is due from the government.
C) No blood money is due; it is هدر.
D) Blood money is due from the person who owns the river.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school ties diyat liability to de facto possession (yad); without possession, the blood is wasted.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
8 min
Liability for a Body at the Riverbank
Ruling that the nearest village owes blood money for a body found entangled at the riverbank

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a dead body found on a riverbank and identify the reasoning (talil) based on de facto possession.

Core Idea
The nearest village owes blood money for a body on its riverbank because their de facto use makes them responsible for that area.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario: a dead body found entangled at the bank of a large river like the Euphrates. The Hanafi school holds the nearest village liable for diyah and qasamah. The ruling is not arbitrary; it is built on the legal principle that the bank is in the effective hand (yad) of those who use it, and thus they have a duty to protect it. We will unpack the reasoning and the distinction from privately-owned rivers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Case and Ruling
A dead body is found entangled at the bank of a large non-owned river. The Hanafi ruling: the nearest village owes qasamah and diyah.
This liability arises from constructive killing (qatl bi al-sabab) due to failure to protect.
2
Examine the Dalil
The bank is considered in the possession (yad) of the nearest village because they use it for drawing water and watering animals. This de facto possession creates a duty to protect that area.
Possession here is effective control, not formal ownership.
3
Trace the Analogy (Qiyas)
The text compares the entangled body to one placed on the bank. The bank is like a place in their hand because they have continuous use. This qiyas establishes that the village is responsible.
The analogy is based on the common illa: effective possession through use.
4
Contrast with a Smaller River
If the river is small and shared (with shuf'ah rights), the river is owned. In that case, liability is on the specific owners, not the nearest village. This distinction shows that the type of possession matters.
Shuf'ah indicates ownership and exclusive control.
5
Identify the Practical Consequence
The ruling forces the village to take security measures or else bear the cost of a killing they failed to prevent. It encourages communal vigilance.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the liability attaches de facto, not de jure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان محتبساً بالشاطيء فهو على أقرب القرى من ذلك المكان على التفسير الذي تقدم لأنه أخص بنصرة هذا الموضع فهو كالموضوع على الشط والشط في يد من هو بقرب منه ألا ترى أنهم يستقون منه الماء ویوردون بهائمهم فيها بخلاف النهر الذى يستحق به الشفعة لاختصاص أهلها به لقيام يدهم عليه فتكون القسامة والدية عليهم.
ترجمہ اور اگر مقتول فرات کے کنارے سے الجھا ہوا ہو تو وہ ( وجوب دیت ) اس جگہ قریبی گاؤں والوں پر ہو گا قرب کی اس تفسیر کے مطابق جو ماقبل میں گزر چکی ہے اس لئے کہ وہی ( اقرب القرمی اس جگہ کی نصرت کے ساتھ مخصوص ہے تو مقتول ایسا ہو گیا جیسے کنارے پر رکھا ہوا اور کنارہ ان لوگوں کے قبضہ میں ہے جو اس سے قریب میں کیا آپ نہیں دیکھتے کہ وہ اہل اقرب القریٰ اس سے پانی بھرتے ہیں اور اس میں اپنے چوپاؤں کو لاتے ہیں بخلاف اس نہر کے جس کے ذریعہ شفعہ کا استحقاق ہوتا ہے شفعہ کے مختص ہونے کی وجہ سے اس نہر کے ساتھ اس نہر پر ان کا قبضہ ہو نیکی وجہ سے تو قسامت اور دیت انہی پر ہوگی۔ تشریح اگر مقتول بڑے دریا کے کنارے سے الجھا ہوا ہے تو جو بستی اس جگہ کے قریب ہوگی انہی پر دیت واجب ہوگی کیونکہ اس جگہ کی نصرت انہیں کا فریضہ ہے تو ان کی جانب سے تقصیر کا صدور ہوا ہے اس لئے وہ دیت کے ذمہ دار ہوں گے اور قرب کی تفسیر ماقبل میں گذر چکی ہے تو اب اس مقتول کا حکم ایسا ہو گیا جیسے دریا کے کنارے پر پڑے ہوئے کا اور کنارہ چونکہ اس بستی والوں کے قبضہ میں ہے کیونکہ وہ اس کنارے سے پانی لیتے ہیں اور اپنے چوپاؤں کو یہاں لاتے ہیں ہاں اگر چھوٹی نہر ہو جس میں شرکت کی وجہ سے شفعہ کا استحقاق ہوتا ہے تو وہ نہر اس کے اہل کے قبضہ میں ہے لہذا اگر اس میں کوئی مقتول میلا تو اس کی دیت انہی پر واجب ہوگی اور انہی پر قسامت واجب ہوگی۔
And if he (the slain) is entangled at the bank, then it is upon the nearest village of that place, according to the interpretation that preceded, because it (the nearest village) is more specific in aiding this place, so it is like being placed on the bank, and the bank is in the hand of those near it. Do you not see that they draw water from it and bring their animals to it? Unlike the river for which preemption is claimed because of the specialization of its people with it due to their hand over it. So the qasamah and diyah are upon them. Translation: And if the slain is entangled at the bank of the Euphrates, the obligation of blood money is upon the nearest village of that place according to the preceding interpretation, because they are specifically responsible for aiding this place, so it is as if the slain is placed on the bank, and the bank is in the possession of those near it. Do you not see that the people of the nearest village draw water from it and bring their cattle to it? Unlike the river for which preemption is claimed, because the river is specialized to its people due to their hand over it. So qasamah and diyah are upon them. Explanation: If the slain is entangled at the bank of a large river, the nearest village is liable because they are entrusted with guarding that place, and they failed, so they must pay. The interpretation of proximity was given earlier. The ruling is like that of a body on the bank, and the bank is in their possession as they use it. However, if it is a small river for which shuf'ah is claimed due to shared ownership, then that river is in the possession of its owners, so if a slain is found there, blood money is upon them and qasamah is upon them.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage specifies the ruling for a dead body found entangled at the bank of a large, non-owned river such as the Euphrates. The Hanafi position is that the nearest village bears the responsibility of qasamah and diyah. The reasoning (talil) is that the bank is in the de facto possession (يد) of the people of the nearest village because they use it for drawing water and watering animals. Thus, they are considered the guardians of that spot. Their failure to prevent a killing makes them constructively liable. The passage distinguishes this from a small, privately-owned river where shuf'ah (preemption rights) apply. In that case, the owners have more exclusive possession, so they alone are liable. This distinction shows that liability is tied to actual possessory control and the duty to protect.

Key Terms
الشط (al-shaṭṭ): The bank or shore of a river; the area under de facto possession of the nearby residents.
الشفعة (al-shufʿah): Preemption right: the right of a co-owner to buy out a new owner's share. Here used to contrast with the large river scenario.
القسامة (al-qasamah): A collective oath taken by 50 men from the village to swear that they did not kill the victim; if they refuse, they are liable for diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The owner of the river.
B) The government.
C) The nearest village because they use the bank.
D) No one, because the river is not owned.

Key Takeaway
Liability for a body on a riverbank is based on de facto possession and use, not formal ownership.
